          <h1 class="large-header mbl top" id="galleryTitleDisplay" itemprop="headline">
           Build a Backyard Fish Pond Without Going Belly Up
          </h1>
         </div>
         <h2 class="small-header top" id="gallerySubtitleDisplay" itemprop="alternativeHeadline">
          You can make an outdoor fish paradise of your own, for less than you might think. But you'll need this expert design wisdom
         </h2>
         <meta content="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/8291e1270bec5a48_3-2360/traditional-landscape.jpg" itemprop="image"/>
        </div>

        <div class="viewGalleryDescriptionHolder" itemprop="description">
         <div class="viewGalleryDescription " id="viewGalleryDescription">
          One of the most memorable times of my life was the week I spent in Hawaii for my brothers wedding. The resort on the beach, the sunshine, the warm weather, the mai tais and
          <a href="http://www.drinksmixer.com/drink4773.html" rel="nofollow" target="_blank">
           Lava Flows
          </a>
          , morning swims in the ocean, sitting next to a waterfall, watching the sunset as a grass-skirted man danced and sliced coconuts with a giant machete  it was all so serene and charming.
          <br/>
          <br/>
          But perhaps what completed the tropical experience most was the abundance of shimmering koi ponds almost everywhere I stepped. Glimmering white, orange and black fish the size of party subs were right at my fingertips. To that effect, for me anyway, I find that the most peaceful, tranquil, relaxing and inspiring home landscapes are those that incorporate a fish pond.
          <br/>
          <span>
          </span>
          <br/>
          So I wondered: What does it take to put a fish pond in a backyard? Harold Leidner was able to offer guidance. The Dallas landscape designer has built dozens of fish ponds in every shape and size, all the way up to a lavish $40,000 koi pond the size of a swimming pool.
          <br/>
          <br/>
          The takeaway is, like indoor aquariums, fish ponds are not for the faint of heart. They take a lot of time and effort, love and care. They can be time consuming, budget draining and, at times, heartbreaking (losing any pet is tough). But as with most difficult things in life, the payoff is worth it. And if youve got the time  even just one free weekend  Leidner says you can build your own backyard fish pond for about $2,500.
          <br/>
          <br/>
          Heres what you'll need to consider.
         </div>
        </div>
        <div class="out" id="gallerySpaces" itemprop="articleBody" lb-context="gallery_8110240" scopeid="gallerySpaces">
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="53445" objid="53445" posid="0">

            </div>

          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="53445">
            <span>
             <b>
              1. Dig
             </b>
            </span>
            <br/>
            <span>
             <br/>
             Youre going to need a hole. A big one.
            </span>
            <span>
             It's free if you do it yourself. If you need the dirt hauled away, youll have to pay. Prices typically range from a couple hundred dollars to several hundred, depending on the amount and other factors.
            </span>
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Leidner prefers deeper holes, but its up to you what aesthetic youre going for. Both shallow and deep ponds have their benefits and disadvantages.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="219003" objid="219003" posid="1">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">

            <div>

            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/219003/exedra-architects-contemporary-landscape-london">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>

           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">

             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="219003">
            <b>
             Advantages of Shallower Ponds
            </b>
            <br/>
            <span>
             <br/>
            </span>
            <span>
             Less excavation means less work and fewer materials, so youll save money on construction costs and backaches.
            </span>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="112195" objid="112195" posid="2">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/112195/Exterior-midcentury-landscape-orange-county">
            <div>
             <img alt="Midcentury Landscape by Laidlaw Schultz architects" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="552" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'112195');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/71f12c780d3e25c1_2267-w422-h552-b0-p0--midcentury-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/112195/Exterior-midcentury-landscape-orange-county">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/lsarchitects/laidlaw-schultz-architects">

              Laidlaw Schultz architects
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="112195">
            At 1 foot to 18 inches, a shallow pond will allow you a better view of your fish.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="2286139" objid="2286139" posid="3">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/2286139/Kuikawa-Lot-1-tropical-landscape-hawaii">
            <div>
             <img alt="Tropical Landscape by GM Construction, Inc." class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="634" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'2286139');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/4021c4a000d4fb36_1736-w422-h634-b0-p0--tropical-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/2286139/Kuikawa-Lot-1-tropical-landscape-hawaii">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/gmconstructioninc1/gm-construction-inc">
             <span class="pro-name">
              GM Construction, Inc.
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="2286139">
            Water plants like lily pads will thrive at this depth also. They can't grow in deep water.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="1347169" objid="1347169" posid="4">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/1347169/Zen-koi-floating-steps-beach-plantings-bungalow-contemporary-landscape-san-diego">
            <div>
             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by The Design Build Company" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="562" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'1347169');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/05111a90002351a2_1951-w422-h562-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/1347169/Zen-koi-floating-steps-beach-plantings-bungalow-contemporary-landscape-san-diego">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/roberthill/the-design-build-company">
             <span class="pro-name">
              The Design Build Company
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="1347169">
            Smaller ponds obviously take up less space and can lend themselves to small design flourishes that make a big statement.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="73812" objid="73812" posid="5">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/73812/Wang-Residence-asian-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>
             <img alt="Asian Landscape by Ami Saunders, MLA" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="634" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'73812');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/7f618e340caf8d1c_2306-w422-h634-b0-p0--asian-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/73812/Wang-Residence-asian-landscape-other-metro">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/amisaunders/ami-saunders-mla">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Ami Saunders, MLA
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="73812">
            <b>
             Advantages of Deeper Ponds
            </b>
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Fish will grow only in relation to how big the space is that theyre in. In shallow water a koi fish, which can reach 3 feet in length, wont reach its full potential, as it would in a 5-foot-deep pond.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Fish tend to get sicker in small ponds, because theyre swimming around in water with higher concentrations of their own waste. Deeper ponds mean more water volume and cleaner water. The fish are healthier, making it easier to maintain the right chemical balance.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Deeper ponds make fish harder targets for predators like birds, raccoons and cats, which can easily swipe, swoop and make dinner of fish in shallower ponds. Letting predators make dinner of your fish obviously wouldnt be cool, especially if you drop $5,000 on one koi fish.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="2542440" objid="2542440" posid="6">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/2542440/31-Albion-Place-asian-landscape-other-metro">
            <div>
             <img alt="Asian Landscape by Teri Fotheringham Photography" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'2542440');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/7461976a00fde429_1672-w606-h454-b0-p0--asian-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/2542440/31-Albion-Place-asian-landscape-other-metro">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/terifoth/teri-fotheringham-photography">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Teri Fotheringham Photography
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="2542440">
            <span>
             <b>
              2. Line the Hole
             </b>
             <br/>
             <br/>
            </span>
            Surprise: Dirt holes dont retain water very well. You'll have to line your depression. The three most popular methods are concrete spray, rubber liners and plastic tubs.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="742416" objid="742416" posid="7">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,543" data-stnd="606,441">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742416/English-Tudor-Estate-and-Garden-traditional-landscape-dallas">
            <div>
             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by Harold Leidner Landscape Architects" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="441" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'742416');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/58e1bcac0f74ec3f_2052-w606-h441-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742416/English-Tudor-Estate-and-Garden-traditional-landscape-dallas">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/haroldleidner/harold-leidner-landscape-architects">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Harold Leidner Landscape Architects
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="742416">
            Gunite is a spray concrete commonly used to line swimming pools. Leidner used it for this expansive fish pond in Dallas, built for around $25,000.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            <span>
             <b>
              Pros:
             </b>
             Gunite is long lasting and durable. Creative lighting and other nifty design elements are easy to include.
            </span>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="742412" objid="742412" posid="8">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,543" data-stnd="606,441">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742412/English-Tudor-Estate-and-Garden-traditional-landscape-dallas">
            <div>
             <img alt="Traditional Landscape by Harold Leidner Landscape Architects" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="441" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'742412');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/1591c24f0f74ec26_6546-w606-h441-b0-p0--traditional-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/742412/English-Tudor-Estate-and-Garden-traditional-landscape-dallas">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/haroldleidner/harold-leidner-landscape-architects">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Harold Leidner Landscape Architects
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="742412">
            <b>
             Cons:
            </b>
            Cost. Youll need a professional to install gunite. Leidner says a feasible budget for a professional installation of an average-size pond is $5,000 minimum, which includes pump parts and labor but not fish.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="82186" objid="82186" posid="9">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/82186/Garden-Pond-Rydal-Pa-eclectic-landscape-philadelphia">
            <div>
             <img alt="Eclectic Landscape by Garrett Churchill Inc." class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'82186');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/b541053b0cd456c1_2316-w606-h454-b0-p0--eclectic-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/82186/Garden-Pond-Rydal-Pa-eclectic-landscape-philadelphia">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/asykes/garrett-churchill-inc">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Garrett Churchill Inc.
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="82186">
            Rubber liners are also common. Strips are rolled out, sealed together and used to line an excavated hole. Rocks and other design elements are added on top and then filled with water.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            <b>
             Pros:
            </b>
            They are common and fairly inexpensive. You can purchase a prepackaged pond kit with rubber liners for $850 to $1,500, depending on the size and other factors.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="478098" objid="478098" posid="10">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/478098/Private-Garden-Spaces-mediterranean-landscape-san-francisco">
            <div>
             <img alt="Mediterranean Landscape by www.KarlGercens.com" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="562" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'478098');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/82016bf80f18df06_2145-w422-h562-b0-p0--mediterranean-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/478098/Private-Garden-Spaces-mediterranean-landscape-san-francisco">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/karl-gercens/wwwkarlgercenscom">
             <span class="pro-name">
              www.KarlGercens.com
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="478098">
            <b>
             Cons:
            </b>
            Rubber liners are fairly difficult to install; not all homeowners will understand how to glue and seam the liners together. A small mistake can result in a big drainage problem, and youll lose your water and fish.
            <span>
             Burrowing rodents also can nibble through the liner. Youd be surprised how quickly a koi pond will drain into the ground, Leidner says.
            </span>
            <br/>
            <br/>
            <b>
             Expert tip:
            </b>
            If you do go with a rubber liner, Leidner recommends buying a few bags of concrete, poking holes in them and using the bags to line the hole. Then spray the bags with water and put the rubber liner on top. The bag will dissolve and the concrete will harden, creating a barrier that rodents cant get through.
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Premade plastic tubs are a good alternative. They start at around $800 but come in limited shapes and sizes.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="1516356" objid="1516356" posid="11">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/1516356/Spec-home-goes-custom-eclectic-exterior-new-york">
            <div>
             <img alt="Eclectic Exterior by Liquidscapes" class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="570" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'1516356');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/ddf166760044e84d_3342-w422-h570-b0-p0--eclectic-exterior.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/1516356/Spec-home-goes-custom-eclectic-exterior-new-york">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/liquidscapes/liquidscapes">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Liquidscapes
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="1516356">
            <b>
             3. Install the Pump Equipment
            </b>
            <br/>
            <span>
             <br/>
            </span>
            <span>
             Pump equipment can be purchased at most local pool and pond supply companies, with complete installation instructions.
            </span>
            You can hide it under rocks or plants, or purchase extensions to conceal it on the side of your house.
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container portrait " lb-sid="53447" objid="53447" posid="12">
          <div class="gallery-photo trackMe">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/53447/Outdoor-Living-with-Water-Gardens-rustic-landscape-chicago">
            <div>
             <img alt="Rustic Landscape by Aquascape Inc." class="hide-context " data-pin-no-hover="true" height="634" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'53447');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/9211d9b60bec5a58_1000-w422-h634-b0-p0--rustic-landscape.jpg" width="422"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/53447/Outdoor-Living-with-Water-Gardens-rustic-landscape-chicago">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/aquascapeinc/aquascape-inc">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Aquascape Inc.
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="53447">
            <b>
             4. Add Landscaping
            </b>
            <br/>
            <br/>
            Having a hole with water won't do anything for your
            <i>
             chi.
            </i>
            Make sure you budget for rocks, plants or decking.
            <span>
             Once you've got everything looking pretty, add water. You'll want to check the chemical balance and temperature before adding fish.
            </span>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
         <div class="gallery-photo-container landscape " lb-sid="880606" objid="880606" posid="13">
          <div class="gallery-photo reloadMe trackMe" data-full="746,559" data-stnd="606,454">
           <a compid="img" href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/880606/Chevy-Chase-contemporary-landscape-los-angeles">
            <div>
             <img alt="Contemporary Landscape by Behr Construction Company" class="hide-context reloadable" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="454" lb-mag="" lb-view="" oncontextmenu="PhotoContextMenu.show(event,'880606');return false;" ondragstart="return false" onmousedown="preventImageDrag(event)" onselectstart="return false" src="http://st.houzz.com/fimgs/20414dab0fa30533_2037-w606-h454-b0-p0--contemporary-landscape.jpg" width="606"/>
            </div>
           </a>
           <div class="space-link tiny-text" compid="more">
            <a href="http://www.houzz.com/photos/880606/Chevy-Chase-contemporary-landscape-los-angeles">
             <span class="more-icon">
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
           <div class="gallery-photo-credit tiny-text" compid="credit">
            <a class="hz-username" href="http://www.houzz.com/pro/behrbuilding/behr-construction-company">
             <span class="pro-name">
              Behr Construction Company
             </span>
            </a>
           </div>
          </div>
          <div class="gallery-text">
           <div class="thBuzz" hid="880606">
            <span>
             <span>
              <b>
               5. Add Fish
              </b>
             </span>
             <br/>
             <b>
              <span>
              </span>
             </b>
             <br/>
             While koi are the most popular choice for fish ponds, Leidner recommends fancy goldfish. In addition to having ornate patterns and colors almost identical to those of koi, they're less aggressive and less expensive.
             <br/>
             <span>
             </span>
             <br/>
             Koi can range from $200 to $7,000 each, while fancy goldfish go for around $35 each.
            </span>
           </div>
          </div>
         </div>
        </div>

               These are great examples, but even premade ponds can be made to look really great. I wish I had a picture of my pond down south. I started with a 109.99 kit from Sams I made my own waterfall and used natural large rocks and dug and added a bog to the side.  The main thing with ponds is keeping it clean! Checking and cleaning filters daily and keeping algae at bay is super important! "Maintence of ponds" can be costly and need to be considered when adding one. I would definitely recommend comets for first time fish.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               We had a large 'architectural pond' in our previous garden. I designed it myself, but we had it built by a pro. The pond was rubber lined and up to 5' deep. My husband installed a fancy filter system that kept the water so clean we could also swim in it.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               We bought our fish at the local garden center for some euros a piece and they became impressively large over the years. Unfortunately, in the winter of 2009-10 we lost almost all of them due to an extraordinary long period of frost and snow.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               I miss the pond. It was a lot of work to clean it up in the spring and to remove tons of leaves in fall, but it was a joy to look at every day and to watch the wildlife (well, sort of) it attracted - which was also very interesting for the cats.
              </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="siffordgd" data-type="profile">
               Jay Sifford Garden Design
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I've had my pond for about 6 years. It quickly became the focal point of my garden, allowing me to build the garden around it. My pond drops off to 4 feet without plant shelves, so predators have a harder time reaching the fish. My pond is also in medium shade, so I have little trouble with algae. I have a reverse osmosis unit in my house. I run the "waste water" through another carbon filter and out to my pond so the pond stays clean and topped off without effort.
               <br/>
               I have found that many people buy cheap filtration equipment. My advice is to buy the best you can afford and you'll never be sorry. I love my skimmer box style filter. The pads are very easy to change.
               <br/>
               As far as an investment in my garden, it was the best $9600 I have spent!
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Maybe you can answer a question that has puzzled me for awhile.  We have hard winters - snow, and the local ponds usually freeze over (but not solid; thick enough some years to skate on, though).  One of the local parks has got what look like very big goldfish in it (probably are goldfish, I guess); the fish are about 12-20 inches long.  The goldfish manage to survive the winter in the pond.  So does that mean if someone put a pond in the garden here, even if it froze over the winter, the fish would survive?
               <br/>
               <br/>
               One poster above said that his/her fish died when there was an unusual frost.  How is it that the fish in my local park survive?  And does it mean I could have a pond in my garden?
              </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="midmodfan" data-type="profile">
               midmodfan
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               scarbowcow: Yes, you could have a pond in your garden! All it takes is enough water that never freezes, i.e. the pond has to be deep, at least partly. Our pond (in Bavaria) was very large and half of it was 5 feet deep - 2 feet more than recommended. But we had large fish and wanted to be sure there was enough water for the ca. three months per year when the pond was frozen and often covered with snow. It worked just fine for more than ten years.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               So, important are the size and depth of the pond (depending on the climate), and the number and size of the fish. The unusual hard and long winter when most of our fish died, there was a mass death of fish even in large natural lakes throughout Northern Europe. That happens from time to time.
              </div>
             </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="ultimatedecor" data-type="profile">
               Sherri Fitzgerald - Ultimate Decor
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Scarbowcow: Yes my pond in the pictures above are in Canada. The pond freezes over in the winter as well. Koi are pretty tough and can survive a normal winter without any trouble. Find out how thick the ice gets during the winter in your area and the pond should be a few feet deeper ... of course the deeper the pond the better chance of survival. The fish stay down low in the pond during the winter. I keep my pump running till the bitter end usually removing it just as the ice is starting to form so the fish have sufficient oxegyn levels.In the spring as early as possible break up the ice so air can get to the fish. My pond is pretty shallow and in a severe winter I have lost some fish but in general if you take care of them they will survive. Other people use water heaters. Here is a few articles that may help...
               <br/>
               http://www.pondtrademag.com/articles/ar-73/
               <br/>
               http://www.lawn-care-academy.com/wintering-koi.html
              </div>

               I agree about the goldfish. My mom has a pond, she did everything herself and bought ordinary goldfish for 50 cents a piece, they live in Edmonton Canada so she has to bring them in, but since she got the pond deep enough she has more of a problem of too many fish than too few ( she gets more every year and has to give them away). They grow huge and she loves them. Most goldfish are super tough and can take anything, and you don't stress out nearly so much if they die as a hundred to thousand dollar koi.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Sure you can spend thousands making the perfect pond, but don't be put off by the high prices quoted. It's also possible on a lower budget- with a bit of determination and time. Here's how we made our backyard pond: we (my husband and sons) dug a hole, and lined it with the proper lining. We covered the bottom with river stones (helps prevent holes and looks great, too). We attached a pump and filter. As recommended by the pet store, we let it run for a couple of days and then added goldfish.

               We built a beautiful pond on our previous property in upstate NY.  We can have very harsh winters.  We used a rubber liner and it was only 20 inches deep with shelves.  We bought 3  6 inch koi about 6 inches each.  They have grown only a little (maybe 8 inches) due to the size of the pond.  But they were beautiful and so much fun!  We did NOT use a heater for the winter, but just used a pump for the winter so the pond would not completely freeze over.  A big cleanup in the Spring, but it was my blissful oasis all the years we were there.

            <span class="anchorPoint" id="c21">
            </span>
            <a class="hlAnchor" name="160441">
            </a>
            <div class="flag-message text-center">

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile">
               peetsoo
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               scarbowcow - yes you can overwinter fish but to do it properly you have to have the right conditions.   I live in Northern Ontario where we have winter October to April and our fish have survived 4 winters sucessfully.   Our pond is 4 feet deep and we run a bubbler (airstone) all winter and keep a small heater going as well (the heater does not heat the pond but simply keeps a small area ice free so that there can be gas exchange - eliminatining probably the biggest risk of fish loss).    We have also gone to the trouple of covering the pond with a dome (forming a greenhouse of sorts and serving to shelter from cold winds) as an extra measure (if the pond was 5 or 6 feet deep we likely wouln't need to bother).    We know of others who don't cover their ponds (of similar depths to ours) in our area  and they have had good sucess also.  One poster suggested breaking the ice in the spring...........(not necessary with the bubbler and /or surface heater) but please don't ever "break" the ice with an axe.  Any pounding  (shock) can kill your fish (by way of swim bladder problems).  If ever a need is there to open up the ice (bubbler stops working)- best to do it gently (drill or even better by pouring hot water (or setting a pot with hot water onto the ice surface).  Sounds like a lot of work (and it can be in our neck of the woods) but well worth it to some of us.
              </div>
              <div class="hzCommentFilesDiv clearfix">
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/ed52b2ea014f0629_9-3420/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/ed52b2ea014f0629_7-3420/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/bde2900a014f0629_9-3420/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/bde2900a014f0629_7-3420/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>

             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="cvaia" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/64b3a5ac0105677d_0-8267/cvaia.jpg"/>
             <div class="hzProIndicator text-center text-bold text-xs-xsmall">
              PRO
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="cvaia" data-type="profile">
               GreenThumb Gardens
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               We built a 20' x 10' pond in our city garden fairly inexpensively about 10 years ago, with a waterfall and a bog area. The waterfall and bog both act as natural filters and bubbler.  We also added a skim box for debris.  The deep area is about 4' deep.  We added fancy goldfish, and get babies each spring.  Since we dug it and built it ourselves, the main cost was for the liner.  A couple of tips, decide where and how big you want it , then increase the size 10-20%.  When done, you will want it bigger.  Dont skimp on liner, keep extra buried around the edge.  Dont just line the edge with stone, make it more natural.  Make the bottom uneven, fish will do better.
               <br/>
              </div>
              <div class="hzCommentFilesDiv clearfix">
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/c1d28ca6014f09fc_9-4400/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/c1d28ca6014f09fc_7-4400/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Yes, Herons and raccoons! I had a Koi pond and really enjoyed it. The raccoons waited until my fish were 3 years old, and then ate them one night. I had an approved metal screen cover over it, and had placed a ladder on top of that to protect them. One night I heard splashing, and ran out in the yard to find all 3 Koi gone in less than a minute.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               I started over with common goldfish in a huge plastic planter. I added a pump and filter, rocks and some aquatic plants. The whole thing cost less than $100. Youtube has great how to videos.

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="tendrich" data-type="profile">
               tendrich
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I love love love our pond! It is much work, especially when things don't go exactly as planned, but, so worth it! You can put a heater in your pond over the winter months.  It keeps a small hole open in the ice, which is just large enough for the gasses (from waste) to escape.  That's supposedly the only reason why you don't want the pond to freeze up.  Our pond is 3-4 feet deep at its deepest.  Depending on placement of rocks, you can discourage animals from wading in and eating your fish.  Also, you can build an underwater bridge where the fish can have refuge.  It's been a learning experience and can be heartbreaking (fish are quite fragile and must have the right ecosystem to thrive).  But, I find it to be one of the most wonderful things I've ever experienced.  I sit at the pond for about an hour every morning in the warmer months...I even get up early so that I have my time to do it!
              </div>
             </div>

             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="webuser_38997" data-type="profile">
               Mary M Withrow
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Hi HappyJackJane, Having a pond in the South or Hot areas I would recommend "the deeper the better" with smooth flowing sides that create a steady flow without dead areas.  You can add a simple harbor, bridge, tree, pond plants or any type of cover to create shade. Put it behind, beside a structure you might have.  Avoid locating your pond on the southern exposure side. I would also add water and stress coat during heat spells and use fish that can take the heat better. Koi like cooler temps but in a fairly large pond they will do fine. These are just ideas I used, hope it helps! Mary
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Hey Happyjackjane ...
               <br/>
               <br/>
               I empathsize with you.  We live on Bribie Island in Queensland Australia and have two reticulating neoprene ponds which we only installed about 2 months ago.  One feeds the other via a couple of small waterfalls. Between them they are only 2 1/2 to 3 feet deep and have a total capacity of about 350 to 400 gallons.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               Since we are geographically opposite in seasons to the States its currently just gone into autum (fall). For what ever reason this year, our summer has been particularly hot with the temperatures seeming to be relentless for weeks on end.  We also have the same problem as you as the two ponds are only about 3 feet deep, which also results in the water being way hotter than I would like, quite often well into the evening.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               I have done a lot of research in an effort to find something that would be cost effective for reducing the temperature with finally just coming back to running the filtration system for much longer periods.  This is simply just in an effort to keep the water circulating and there by (hopefully) to help reduce the effects of heat on the fish.  Besides that .... If anyone has an suggestions I am definitely always open to new ideas ...
              </div>

             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>

             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile">
               peetsoo
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               fatenhappy - if you have Koi in your pond you really need to keep the pump / filter running all 24/7 - cooler water holds more oxygen than warm so you really need to oxegenate the pond in warm weather....and....the bigger the fish, the higher their need for oxygen (particularly with koi).   Oxygen also aids in the constant breakdown of waste as beneficial bacteria need the oxygen to survive and do their job in the pond (an unoxygenated pond can smell very bad  bery quickly when water quality takes a dive).   I wish I had the problem of my yard being too warm.............but I agree with a few of the posters here who state "the deeper the better|".  This holds true in general as a deeper, larger pond does not have temperature fluctuations the way a smaller, shallower one doesand it is rapid fluctuations that are actually what is harmful (In the cool weather the lower depths of a deep pond are warmer than the surface and the reverse holds true in very warm weather.     I know of people who have built a trellis over part of the pond to help with shade and this can prevent temperature swings as well as cut down on agae growth.   Hope this helps........I made many mistakes early on so hoping any of this might be of help to someone just starting out.     Best advice I can give is don't overstock the pond, don't overfeed (this alone leads to many fish deaths and illnesses), put in the best filtration system that you can and don't have rocks on the bottom of the pond (there are mixed opinions on the last suggestion but in my experience there is too much guck that gets caugth in a rock bottom that harbors anarobic bacteria (the kind you don't want) and clean up of a rock bottom pond is nasty (in my experience).    Good luck............a really great hobby!
              </div>
             </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile">
               peetsoo
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               one more thought happyjack jane.   If you have a long and shallow waterfall / stream, it will warm quickly so shutting it down on very hot days may work but only if you ensure that you suppliment oxygenation by using a pump and airstone (or several).  Any shade that you can provide through plant cover or by blocking the sun (trellis) will help............  also, if you use a submersible pump (I do here because a bottom drain would be problematic with our winters) you can raise it off of the bottom (deepest) area of the pond so that the lowest layers of water are not turned over (keeping that area cooler when needed during the hottest time of the day (sliding it back to the bottom in the evening) and in my case, less cool in the cold winter (we turn our pump and filter off altogether in the winter here).
              </div>
             </div>

               teddyg04
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Happyjackjane, I also live in Phoenix.  Make the pond as deep as you feel you can.  Provide plenty of shade for the fish, in the form of rock overhangs, plants, etc.  Planning a way to put a flat rock in the middle, near the surface, is good for shade.  I used a masonry block as a support (put extra liner under it to avoid ripping the liner).  Koi are nice, but Shibunkin goldfish are just as pretty, and very hardy.  Koi need very deep water in hot climates.  Don't forget a uv clarifier; you can get a separate one, or get a pump with one built in.  Keeps the algae under control.
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               If you live in Australia ALWAYS ALWAYS check with your local council as to permits and fencing. My council allows no permit or fencing for a fishpond/pool under 300mm deep beyond that you need a fence, or require it be in a lockable area and with high surrounding fences to stop kiddies drowing. Anything larger you may then have to talk to them about it. Be warned! They can and will make you rip it all out if you do the wrong thing!

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I've always had fish ponds.  When we built our house, we had a huge, ugly hill side that was too steep to mow and only good for growing weeds.  I envisioned a pond with waterfall.  I started by building a retaining wall one summer and added some steps with stones found in the woods on our property. The next spring we had 3 large boulders placed on the hill.  The following summer we had a contractor put in the pond and waterfall.  It has beena work in progress ever since.  I add new plants, shrubs and natural features I find.  A pond is a constant source of work, inspiration, relaxation and joy.
               <br/>
               <br/>

             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="dbomom" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="dbomom" data-type="profile">
               dbomom
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Beautiful photos!  So wish I had seen this article 2 weeks ago.  Has an idea for a waterfall that would have worked beautifully with my pond.  Unfortunately, I just finished another recirculating idea.  I built my pond myself (I'm a 61 year old woman and this is the second one on the same site.)  Just 200 gallons and 1 foot deep, so the fish can survive fairly well even without my pump and filter system--important to me since I had a pump die when I was away on vacation, causing the loss of a number of my fish .  I live in middle Alabama where we've had only 2 days below freezing all winter, so the cold isn't an issue here.  My smaller pond housed my really old goldfish that I brought when we moved from Houston, TX, several years ago.  3 years ago I added some young butterfly koi (3") that grew to 14" in 2 years.  They ate every green plant I put in and added way too much to my tiny filter/pump.  So when I built my new pond last summer, I gave the koi to a friend with a much larger pond.  They are blissfully happy there.
               <br/>
               Extra note here:  In Houston I only had a small container pond sitting on my patio surrounded by bricks.  A snake climbed up and and ate one of my fish. (I killed the snake.)  I also had a raccoon that practically destroyed that little container pond.
               <br/>
               Back to my current pond:  Only partially in ground with raised walls that act as a bench on one side.  I lined the sides with 8x16x4" concrete blocks stacked to make the sides straight since the pond is geometric intersecting rectangles, then placed felt liner and PVC liner, topped with 12x12x2" stepping stones for edging that is large enough for seating.  I also used a couple of 16x16x2" stepping stones in some of the corners to add a tiny amount of overhang and interest in the edging.  I use a 750 gpm submersible pump, a tiny skimmer/filter I bought for around $75, and made a filter from a 5 gallon paint bucket (found instructions on the internet.)  The whole thing cost me around $250.  Koi take more space and better filtration.  Goldfish, expecially those with the long flowing fins are very pretty in ponds, require far less maintenance, are better at avoiding predators, and live a long time.  Some of mine are close to 20 years old.  Also, they don't grow as large as the koi, so a smaller pond will work for them for a much longer time.  The biggest problem I have with my pond is the algae bloom that happens every spring, especially since I cut down the messy Wax Myrtles that had given shade to my smaller pond.  I now get full sun on the pond until noon.  I'm currently building a larger filtration box to hopefully help with that issue since an algae bloom is in my very near future!  I've had a baby raccoon visit my pond, but my neighbor hates all wildlife and had it trapped.  My pond is next to my back door, and I have 2 small barky dogs that run in and out of the house all day.  So I guess most of the predator types prefer to fish from the small lakes that are near our home.  Sorry this is such a long post, but I do love having a small pond.
              </div>
             </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="laurelennis" data-type="profile">
               Laurel Ennis

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               What they don't tell you is that water features attract animals. Skunks, raccoons, rats, mice, bees, wasps, stray animals, coyotes are all attracted to a place to get a drink and/or feed on the fish. Raccoons are notoriously clever and found ways to disconnect the pump and drain the pond then wade in and eat all the fish.  It happened many times no matter how we hid/protected the pump. It's awful to come outside to see the fish and find carnage. It can be very upsetting to kids to find that "Goldie" the fish had been murdered and eaten while they slept. Really something to think about.
              </div>
             </div>

               The only things I did not like were the loud Bull frogs. They are LOUD. if you have a great eco system and I always did, animals come to drink the water because it is clean. They are smart. I never saw a skunk. What I was upset about one year was when the egrets perched on the roof, swooped down and ate all my fish. I was angry. So I bought an 18"  bobble head owl and replaced the fish. The owl scares away the predators.

            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="fatenhappy" data-type="profile">
               fatenhappy
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Point taken Laurel re the "freeloaders" that can come to lunch ... Here we have a major problem with Egrets ... I don't know if you are at all or are familiar this type of bird, but these guys specialise in stealing fish... Almost styled and tailor made for the job ....
               <br/>
               <br/>
               From the first moment our two ponds went into the ground a little while back, these particular birds have always proven to be a nuisance.  We simply did not want an ugly artificial man-made cover over the area that would upset the aesthetics.  As a compromise, in the shorter term I have made a light, slightly elevated mesh (temporary) cover, but only for the particular pond that has our fish.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               in the mean time we have placed a few Lilly pads that are propagating very rapidly and maturing nicely, so much so I am very happy to report, we are almost to the stage that the screen will be able to be removed altogether.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               To me, sometimes life just has to be a compromise.  In our case it's always been about getting these two small ponds up and running sustainably so the eventual aesthetics for that part of the garden will be more gratifying.
              </div>

               Laurie
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I've enjoyed looking at all the photos of everyone's ponds.  :)
               <br/>
               <br/>
               The author made a mistake in the article though.  Fish will only grow to the size of their tank/pond if the water is never changed.  They emit a hormone that will keep their growth in check.  When you do water changes on the pond you remove those hormones and the fish will continue to grow.  It's not so much the depth of the pond, but the number of gallons that should determine the fish you have.  You could have an 18" deep large pond that has more gallons to it than a 5' deep tiny pond.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               If you pond is small consider using goldfish.  They can get quite large and come in a variety of patterns too.  The big chain stores have them for pennies and sometimes the more fancy ones get mixed in and you can get a real bargin.
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Re' Trishabritt 1:
               <br/>
               Not sure what kind of garden tub you have.  It can be buried or sit on concrete.  The only thing to be wary of, though, is galvanized tin.  You will have to line anything made of that material with plastic.  Not sure what the issue is, but it causes fish to lose their slime coating and they die.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Paulette50 - just wanted to mention that feeding fish over the winter months in cold climates is not necessary and in fact should not be done as it can kill them as their metabolisms slow right down so any food that they do injest may not get processed and can do a fish harm......feeding should only resume once the water temperature warms......uneaten food will just foul the water......
               <br/>

               Had a small backyard kidney shaped preformed pond for about ten years. I loved it and loved having fish and a small burbler. Unfortunately even in our urban setting (San Francisco) the raccoons made my yard a regular stop on their neighborhood marauding. Only the most clever fish survived but they all would succumb eventually (i even had a little hide area for them). the worst part was that even after i stopped having fish they would come and dismantle the pump, fling bits of the filter around and regularly drain it. i finally dug it up last year for an above ground fountain. I was told by a landscaper that three feet was minimum to keep the raccoons from wading in.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Raccoons are a clever lot. Even here in suburban Scottsdale, they got into my pond last year and killed several of my fish.  I removed the survivors and emptied the pond, and completely redid it.  Fewer rocks on the bottom, more hides, etc.  4" pvc sections are good hides, as well as a masonry block on end, supporting a flagstone.  No problems since.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               We have had a garden pond for 8 years and never tire of hearing the water falls and watching the fish.
               <br/>
               We have a combination of koi, gold fish, comets, shebunkins (calico) fish. Currently we have around 50 fish. Have had black moors but they have never survived. Live in Ohio so have winters but it's 2 feet deep with one end gradually shallower. Going to pull up all the lilies this year as they have spread too much. Will now just have all plants in pots set on shelves to keep them under control. Our only trouble was the first 3 years we tried all the chemicals to keep it PH balanced. Spent couple hundred dollars each summer. Stopped doing that and now we just add barley bales to it and have had no problem keeping it clear. Grandkids will get in and splash around and the fish just get out of they way. LOVE LOVE IT! We did all the work ourselves. Hauled the stones from our woods. Reading all of the comments have helped with ideas and given us solutions. Thanks!
              </div>
             </div>

               Another cost to consider, depending upon where you live, rocks can get expensive.  I do love my little waterfall pond.  I was fortunate in that a retaining wall at the small garden behind the house enabled me to have a small waterfall with the recirculating filter/pump.  My only problem has been algae which was imported with some gifted water flowers that came from a lake.  I've been fighting the algae for the last year which at times has over whelmed my filter/pump.  In our warm Florida winters with the windows open I can hear the waterfall, it's very peaceful.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               There are several good companies that make pumps with built in uv lights, or you can add a uv clarifier to your setup if there's room.  I didn't have room for a separate clarifier, so the pump with the built in uv was a lifesaver (not to mention a pond saver). I had tried everything to get rid of the algae, and this did the trick.

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="lptboyer" data-type="profile">
               lptboyer
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I have a totally different outlook on this.  We live on the side of a mountain in Virginia.  We only own 11 acres, but are surrounded on all sides by up to 100 acres on one side of our property.  Our "backyard" was literally the dug out side of a mountain, nothing would grow.  So my husband and I decided to put in retaining walls and a waterfall/pond.  My comment is not about the construction though, it is what has occurred since we put in this large water feature.  We now regularly have copperheads, tons more mice (which are probably drawing the copperheads), hundreds, literally hundreds of frogs every spring, which leave their slimy strings of eggs and then millions of babies hatching every year.  We have more insects than you could imagine as well.  But we lived here for 5 years before we added the pond and had none of these problems until about the 2nd year after we had built the waterfall and pond.  Does no-one else have these issues?  I will say it is beautiful, but fear of copperheads hiding in the rocks keeps me from ever wanting to sit out and enjoy it with my two small grandchildren.  If anyone has any advice, I would love to hear it! These are things that I have never heard anyone else complaining about when talking about water features.
              </div>
             </div>

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="teddyg04" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>

            <div class="comment-actions">

            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="teddyg04" data-type="profile">
               teddyg04
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Water lilies do well in full sun, and provide shade for fish.  Fancy goldfish (shibunkins, Sarassa comets) are easy to keep and very hardy, as well as pretty.  As I have written before, either a uv clarifier between filter and pond or a pump with built-in uv will do wonders for keeping algae at bay.  Don't make the same mistake I originally did  and put too much rock on the bottom; it only creates a great place for algae and sludge to build up.  A thin layer of small river pebbles will do just fine.
              </div>
             </div>
             <div class="commentInfo tiny-text">

            <a class="hlAnchor" name="183278">

            <div class="flag-message text-center">
             Thank you for reporting this comment.
             <a class="flag-comment-action colorLink unflag" href="javascript:">

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="natural_ponds" data-type="profile" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/3e83aeec01560046_0-0663/natural_ponds.jpg"/>
             <div class="hzProIndicator text-center text-bold text-xs-xsmall">
              PRO
             </div>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="natural_ponds" data-type="profile">
               Spring Creek Aquatic Concepts
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I'd like to offer a bit of a different perspective on backyard fish ponds.  The author could be more specific when he says fish ponds take time and effort and they are not for the faint of heart.  Since this article is really about bargain priced ponds, I would tend to agree only for bargain ponds.  At the same time I would not want to discourage those interested in investing in an actual care-free pond or stream.  I believe Houzz readers are interested in quality as well as budget.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               The reader responses found here affirm budget ponds require more effort and operating expense.  The return from more scientific design are realized not only in ease of function of the aquatic feature, but also a more natural aesthetic.  A pond that "feels" more natural tends to look more attractive for reasons that seem more instinctual rather than rational.  A few designers are aware of fine details that lend to the most natural feel.  The client may never be able to put those design elements into words, but  they can be felt in the full experience of encountering waters that seem much more natural.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               Not everyone is going to sense what is natural.  But for those who can make the distinction, a bargain project will just not feel right.  Its aesthetics may even irritate the eye of an old fisherman who has seen many natural waters.  It really is about the eye of the beholder.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               As far as the rules of building ponds,  as the designer's acumen increases,  those rules melt away.  In our work, we have successfully broken every rule found in these articles - especially the large fish don't grow in small ponds rule.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               Flora in images:  seep monkeyflower ( yellow ) ,  small flower forget-me-not ( blue ) ,  Indian paintbrush ( red )
              </div>
              <div class="hzCommentFilesDiv clearfix">
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/6b02b0a20189e95f_9-2674/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/6b02b0a20189e95f_7-2674/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Fatenhappy - I like your pond cover! My friend made a similar one for a smaller pond after raccoons ate an $8,000 koi. His is plastic mesh and he installed it a couple inches under the surface of the water. Between reflections and water movement, you don't even see it unless you're looking for it. He does have to take it out to scrub it every once in awhile but it works well for a very small pond, and he has not lost a single fish since it went in.

               crnaguy

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               about 20 years ago here in central illinois, we had an above ground pool.  i was also big into aquariums.  i had heard that goldfish could overwinter in a pool cover if it didn't freeze.  after the first rain i bought 20 feeder goldfish (  just like it sounds for bigger aquarium fish )  i put them on top of the pool cover and did nothing else. they only ate what fell into the pool cover.   next spring i found 17 of them that had grown at least 4 times bigger.
              </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="mselby562" data-type="profile">
               mselby562
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Koi will mature to their full sizee no matter what the size if your pond and are more susceptible to disease if the chemical balance is off. They require at least 50 gallons per fish so will not survive well in a small place. At our local pond supply store the sales person told me you may end up with fish on the lawn if the pond is too small. Gold fish only need about 20 tp 30 gallons per fish. Both fish hibernate in winter and can survive under ice if your pond has enough volume. We lived in a house in Newport RI, and the ice on the fish pond was 6 inches thick for 2 months, there were 30 fish and they all survived...fancy goldfish, the pond was 6 feet deep. We just finished our fish pond here in VA and I released 9 fish into it yesterday. So excited.
              </div>
              <div class="hzCommentFilesDiv clearfix">
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/0532f64b01c1a933_9-6266/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/0532f64b01c1a933_7-6266/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>
               <img class="cursor-magnify js-enlarge img-responsive" data-imgurl="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/34b2fac201c1a937_9-6270/home-design.jpg" data-pin-no-hover="true" height="190" src="http://st.houzz.com/simgs/34b2fac201c1a937_7-6270/home-design.jpg" width="240"/>
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Everything I've read recommends not doing concrete ponds in very cold climates.  A top quality liner with good padding might be a less expensive and longer-lasting way to go.  Even in warmer climates, concrete ponds are known to develop cracks and leaks, which are much harder to repair than leaks in a lined pond.
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I have read through this entire thread and am happy to know that there are so many enthusiasts still learning the way.  I began my pond over a year ago and decided it was too small.  It was about 300 gallons.  I knew it needed to be larger and situated differently, but was unsure about how to go about it.  After 6 months of research, I decided to take "the plunge".  My new pond is 16 feet long and 8 feet wide, 3 feet deep at the deepest point with two falls, a stream bed and a bog filter.  I am adding another stream bed and filter fall this weekend to "up" my filtration capacity.  Right now, we're at approximately 1,800 gallons!  Thanks to the inspiration of people like yourselves, great things can be accomplished!
              </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="peetsoo" data-type="profile">
               peetsoo
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Here in northern Ontario we shut the pumps/filters down in the winter and just run an airstone (and a floating heater to keep an area free of ice).    This helps to keep the less cold water at the bottom of the pond where the fish are and prevents the water from super-cooling............works well as long as there is a deep area in the pond and the water is good quality heading into the winter.
              </div>
             </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="tassietiger" data-type="profile">
               tassietiger
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Hi lptboyer, you betcha the pond is what is bringing the snakes.  I live in Tasmania and we are very aware that if you have a water source in your garden (a dripping tap is enough) you have the potential for a snake visit.    We have a nature reserve across the road from us and snakes have been seen crossing the road to visit our yards.  We have principally tiger and copperhead snakes here, both very nasty if you get bitten. I have had bluetongue lizards (big fat fellows that are about 12 plus inches long) that are very welcome as they are non aggressive and eat the snails (so no snail bait permitted in my yard!) and my strawberries, They visit the pond for water so am sure that snakes would have been around as well, particularly as I have lots of ferns and the deck is raised a bit, providing great hiding places.  It is a myth that if you have blutongues in your garden you won't have snakes.  I avoid gardening in the summer and only carry out what is necessary - very cautiously! . I am renting and the garden came with a half full, stinky, slimy, small plastic pond, breeding mosquitoes right beside the deck.  I scrubbed it out, put in a water lily in a pot, and a couple very small goldfish, about $2 each from the pet store.  They thrived and grew huge,  I just stick the hose in occasionally and flush the pond.  If it gets algae I use a product from aristopet (Queensland product) called aquamaster.  All these algal things should only be used after reading the instructions carefully.  If you can't work out the volume of your pond just Google the question like I did.  Because the pond is quite small though a scrub with a bit of broken broom head and then a flush with the garden hose works a treat.  It has rid us of the mosquitoes.  Although very near a beach I haven't had any problems with seagulls hunting the fish - a section of the pond is overhung by a bush.  The first thing the grandchildren ask as they rush in for a visit is "can we feed the fish Nanna?"  They spend ages perched on the side of the pond just watching quietly.  The little pond gives us all a great deal of pleasure.
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               The design of the pond is important. The usual design is for it to slope towards the drainage part. Fish feces then gather in one area and the extraction pump end should be at the lowest point. Hence when you clean the pond the muck is sucked out. Learned this the hard way. If not done this way ammonia builds up and fouls the water.
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               How small is your yard?  I live in an opposite climate (desert Southwest), but understand that with a deicer to keep a small opening for oxygen transfer, a pond is very doable.  Everything I've read says to build as large as is possible.  It really is easier for maintenance and fish health.  Goldfish (all varieties) are cold-water fish, and apparently do well in very cold climates.  Ravens shouldn't be a problem, as long as there are are hiding places for them.  In that vein, deeper is certainly better.  Good luck.  If you do decide to build you won't regret it.
              </div>

             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="mjar" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>

            <div class="comment-actions">

            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="mjar" data-type="profile">
               mjar
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               A bog is a nice companion piece but not a necessity.  A bog provides a natural filtration system using plants which in turn would oxygenate the water and use up nitrates the fish produce.  It would basically be a shallow area connected to the water of your pond which allows for plant material and soil substrates for the plants to be placed in.  If you are an avid gardener it opens up a whole world of plant material to work with that cannot be used in other traditional areas of the garden (climate dependent of course).  I guess it depends upon personal preference if you want to include one but they are definitely not a need.
              </div>
             </div>

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="tassietiger" data-type="profile">
               tassietiger
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               HI, I have loved all the stories, thank you all for sharing your experiences.  I have been interested to see how many times the issue of keeping the water from getting too warm arises.  My small plastic pond has a small area at one end that is about 6" long, not so wide, and about 3" deep.  The small area has a lip between it and the rest of the pond, there is only a skim of water over the top of the lip. The whole pond gets a lot of sun and I used to worry about the water warming.  I have a large goldfish that loves the tiny shallow bit, and it struggles and flips to get over the plastic lip, and it just basks in the direct sun in water that is warm to the touch.   The fish is large and so barely under the water.  When I feed the fish it will just flip over the lip into the bigger part and is invariably back in the shallow bit after it has had a feed. Been lucky so far I guess that a passing seagull or other bird hasn't stolen it.
              </div>
             </div>

               Ok here is a photo of my smallest pond, there is a larger pond above it and a small creek bed.  Here are 2 visitors from the lake across the street.  They do not eat my fish, but Odin, the larger male has trouble getting out.  Freya can be a little mean....but she follows me around.  I have a large fake heron that keeps the herons out.  Has worked pretty well.  And yes, you get quite attached to the fish.  I had one carp who must have been dropped in by a passing bird and he grew to 19".  He used to jump up, and he jumped out and couldn't slide back in.  Had him for 14 years and cried for weeks....

              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="cazzz45" data-type="profile">
               cazzz45
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <br/>
               Am in Australia.
               <br/>
               With daughter's help we built a fish pond that meandered around the already existing tree-ferns, so some parts were deep and other bits quite sallow but still deep enough for the fish to swim some wide areas,some narrow.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               We never had a slime problem and I never feed the fish passed the 1st month.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               First of all the water was shaded from the summer sun from the fronds of the tree ferns also from shade given by a massive wisteria I allowed to spread over the pergola during summer.
               <br/>
               Second as well as a few gold fish I added small native fish the later ate the mosquito larvae so there was never a mosquito issue. the native fish were the key to  the clean pond.
               <br/>
               In one of the deep areas I had a lovely fountain I also added in another place I made a terracotta head to have water spurting from it's mouth all to add oxygen.
               <br/>
               The biggest job was in autumn time removing the wisteria fallen leaves, that was all
               <br/>
               <br/>
               My Setter use to love chasing the goldfish around through the pond then coming out and shaking herself dry near any one there.
               <br/>
               <br/>
               Never for a moment did i think about snakes but I am so glad people have mentioned that as I now live in the countryside you have brought my building of a new pond to a grinding stop. This needs a rethink as the frolicking dog is no more..
               <br/>
               <br/>
               <br/>
              </div>
             </div>
