              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="mjar" data-type="profile">
               mjar
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Leah and Byron
               <br/>
               Don't imagine you built your pond yet and sorry I missed your comment.  One thing you need to worry about is city bylaws.  If your pond is too deep you may have to have a fence around it with a lock on a city lot.  Check your bylaws to be sure.  If you plan on over wintering the fish it is recommended that the minimum depth should be 2 1/2 - 3 feet due to our zone 3.  We did 2 1/2 but benefit from melting Chinooks in Calgary, which I don't remember when I lived in Edmonton!  We use a cattle trough heater to keep a hole in the ice all winter which we used to run to the house on an extension cord but close the pond down to one side in winter.  When we refinished the deck we had an electrician run a power line underground to a post beside the pond which runs our pump and filter in the summer.  We don't cover the pond and only use pond comets as they are better suited to our pond size and climate.
              </div>
             </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I have so enjoyed reading about all the fish-loving and like-minded people.  My tiny pond in Tasmania was being taken over by the potted water lily.  The flowers were lovely, and the plant was flourishing but was starting to fill the pond, meaning the fish had less water for moving around in.  I pulled up the pot and massive plant to put it in a big plastic drum filled with water, and was thrilled and very surprised to see lots of little baby fish in the pond, previously hidden by the lily.  As I only have two mature goldfish in the pond, that I have had since miniscule, I obviously got lucky with a male and female.  Babies are black but will change to gold as they get older.  Trying to work out how to house them as the pond will be overpopulated very quickly as they grow.  Can't have Koi in Tassie, (banned as are tortoises due to our waterways being premium fishing for trout) so when I buy my house I plan to have a wall with a huge koi "diving" into the shower by doing a mosaic.  There are some marvellous tiles out there with metallic backing that should glitter a bit when people enter the backroom at night and turn on the light (which I intend to be a spotlight onto the shower and koi.  Not much worried about making it work, I am lucky enough to be blessed with a bit of arty experience - I do pet portraits in graphite.  I'm looking forward to doing something really dramatic in another medium.  Now I just have to find a house where the shower needs updating......  and then I too cah have a Koi !!!!!!
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               "You can make an outdoor fish paradise of your own, for less than you might think. But you'll need this expert design wisdom" was the point that challenged me to keep reading the article but the comments kept me reading for another hour. I always value and appreciate the experts who present their advice instead of discouraging us DYI folks. My next adventure is expanding my 2 year old plastic experimental pond so that I can grow fish for aquaponics.  Most aquaponics look very industrial -- not aesthetically pleasing.  I don't have 40,000 to pay someone to build the pond that I see in my mind, so I depend on the good graces of other pond enthusiasts to lead the way. Thank you to the writer for posting the article and lovely pics and everyone for your feedback.. Your ponds are lovely.

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               Everyone seems to put a lot of effort into their ponds. My dad is 91 and hasn't been able to do anything to his pond, which is maybe 5' deep and 8' wide. His koi, bought at birth are thriving. He has water lilies and water plants in it there's plenty of algae in it , but never the less with no maintenance  these fish are HUGE now and perfectly fine, eating whatever nature brings their way. One thing my mom would do now and again  is give them oatmeal which they ate up quickly cooked oatmeal at that!  I thought that a little weird , but it works for them.when we did clean the pond it was once a year.. Just thought I'd thro this out there...

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               There is something really bugging me about some of these ponds we see in landscapes. Were the builders born in a rock quarry? Maybe they don't know how to do anything else, but when was the last time you saw a real pond with two-and-a-half dump trucks of rock around them? I also understand, yes they get to charge people more money for all that rock. To a person who spends a lot of time around real water, it's just an eyesore! I also think it's all people see so they just accept it. Now I'd hate to see what ponds would look like if the builder was born on a dairy farm ;)
               <br/>
               <br/>
               Most of the time you can't even see the edge of the water on our projects We like native flowering wetlands You know, like those mountain lakes in the calendars.
              </div>

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               I just rebuilt my pond after moles chewed through my liner this past winter and i went a little bigger .I also treated the ground with milky spore to kill the grubs the moles were after .The old pond was 10 years old and the first thing I built at my new built retirement home in 2004 .no grass but a pond .I wanted the pond near the house because I new we were going to build a covered front porch on our home and it had to be half above and half below ground .New liner 145 dollars 2 new laguna pumps 250 dollars and new laguna aerator 127 dollars .Rocks all free from creeks and places i visited and from my own property .Retaining wall block from a person here getting rid of there's for 30 dollars and concrete block free from someones house that was torn down .Banana plants from my sister and giant turtle from my sister .I now have grass and planted and did everything myself and it is 3 acres of land I had with only dirt and no trees .30 trees later and lots of work in 10 years .Not sure I would call it retirement .But now I love all my work

              <div class="commentBodyText">
               if you put plants in your filter like mine it is like a mini bog .My filters are stock tanks I got at a house auction 20 dollars for 2 of them .I put grasses in pots and added them to my lava rock filters .I can't have plants in my pond because the koi destroy them .They are over 2 ft long now .Pond is around 4000 gallons with 2 filters no bottom drain and no skimmer .My koi I got in NC at a koi farm 3 dollars and my other fish were 1 dollar a piece and I have given lots of babies away

            <div class="flag-message text-center">
             Thank you for reporting this comment.

            <div class="feedUThumb pull-left mrm">
             <img class="hzHouzzer hzHCUserImage userImage hz-user-image" data-id="robert6262" data-type="profile" src="http://www.houzz.com/res/2199/pic/user_0.png?v=2199"/>
            </div>
            <div class="comment-actions">
            </div>
            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="robert6262" data-type="profile">
               Robert
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               We live in south Louisiana and have had a 18" deep koi pond for 9 years. It is rubber lined with a layer of river rocks. Three times the pond has survived hurricanes with tons of debris and loss of power for days. We regret not making it deeper, but all of our koi have survived. For those of you wondering what the best way to keep predators away we have had great success with a device called the Scarecrow. It is a motion detector situated at the edge of the pond that is attached to your water supply. It can shoot water up to 30' away. Requires a 9 volt battery. Our neighbor gifted it to us after losing a fish years ago to a Blue Heron. That device and having made the decision to spend more money on the initial filtration system I believe has been the main reason for its success.
              </div>
             </div>

            <div class="commentBody">
             <div class="commentBodyContent">
              <span class="hz-username hzHouzzer hzHCUserName " data-id="rebekah_picot" data-type="profile">
               Rebekah Picot
              </span>
              <div class="commentBodyText">
               <p>
                Important factor to consider; mosquitos
               </p>
               <p>
                We built our pond several years ago from a liner kit and weren't planning on stocking it with fish, because of it's small size.  It wasn't long before we noticed mosquito larva even with the pump and filter.  Didn't want to put chemicals in it so went to the pet store and picked up 20 feeder fish for $6. Many of them were pretty calico colors and eventually grew to 4-6 inches long and even had babies.  Never had a problem with mosquitos and a few of the fish even lived for 3 years, until one especially harsh winter.
               </p>
              </div>
             </div>

The resort on the beach, the sunshine, the warm weather, the mai tais and Lava Flows, morning swims in the ocean, sitting next to a waterfall, watching the sunset as a grass-skirted man danced and sliced coconuts with a giant machete \u2014 it was all so serene and charming. But perhaps what completed the tropical experience most was the abundance of shimmering koi ponds almost everywhere I stepped. Glimmering white, orange and black fish the size of party subs were right at my fingertips. To that effect, for me anyway, I find that the most peaceful, tranquil, relaxing and inspiring home landscapes are those that incorporate a fish pond. So I wondered: What does it take to put a fish pond in a backyard? Harold Leidner was able to offer guidance. The Dallas landscape designer has built dozens of fish ponds in every shape and size, all the way up to a lavish $40,000 koi pond the size of a swimming pool. The takeaway is, like indoor aquariums, fish ponds are not for the faint of heart. They take a lot of time and effort, love and care. They can be time consuming, budget draining and, at times, heartbreaking (losing any pet is tough). But as with most difficult things in life, the payoff is worth it. And if you\u2019ve got the time \u2014 even just one free weekend \u2014 Leidner says you can build your own backyard fish pond for about $2,500. Here\u2019s what you'll need to consider. 	function showPostToForumForm (spaceId, ownedByPro, isMP, trackingEventSource) {
		HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.init (spaceId, ownedByPro);
		if(trackingEventSource !== undefined) {
			HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.setTrackingEventSource(trackingEventSource);
		}
		if (isMP === undefined) {
			isMP = false;
		}
		if (isMP) {
			HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.setQuestionTopic(HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.PRODUCT_TOPIC);
		}
		else {
			HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.setQuestionTopic(HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog.SPACE_TOPIC);
		}
		HZ.ui.Yamdi.show(HZ.spaceActions.QuestionDialog, null);
	}

    function showSendSpaceEmailDialog(spaceId){
        if (HZ.data &amp;&amp; HZ.data.Spaces) {
            var space = HZ.data.Spaces.get(spaceId);
            if (space) {
                HZ.dialogs.EmailDialog.prepare({
                    type: HZ.sendEmail.types.SPACE,
                    referenceId: space.id,
                    subject: space.t,
                    message: (space.prod ? HZ.sendEmail.texts.DEFAULT_MESSAGE_PRODUCT : HZ.sendEmail.texts.DEFAULT_MESSAGE_SPACE)
                });
                HZ.ui.Yamdi.show (HZ.dialogs.EmailDialog);
            }
        }
    }

		});

	}

		function setUrl_(e){
			url = detokenize(click_data_obj_[e.getAttribute('sid')]);
			e.href = url;
		}		
		
		function detokenize(data) {
			return data[0] + "=" + data[2] + data[1];
		}
